B004A8ZWVK|5.0|\\"The Help\\" works as well as it does because it doesn't offer easy answers. This lovely and sometimes extraordinary film is not concerned with big sermons about clivil rights, religion or easy to swallow messages. The film is simply about behavior; how we should act, morally, towards other people, no matter who they might be. This thematic line is structured nicely throughout the film by zeroing in on a select group of people. This was a smart move since the backdrop of the Civil Rights movement in the 1960s is just too large a subject to truly flesh out in a 2 hour film. Because the film is about actual characters instead of predisposed, prepackaged lessons, the audience can take with them what they choose from the story (none of the characters have a major epiphany, each just starts to become aware of their behavior and effects, both good and bad- they all have so much more life to live). Surprisingly the film rarely gets melodramatic, and when it does it almost seems cheep and easy. Thankfully the films few flaws (structure, pacing, some silly dialogue here and there- \\"You broke her heart...!\\") can be overlooked due to the tremendous performances. Each actress is exceptional in their own ways with the wonderful Viola Davis stealing the film. Emma Stone is reliable here as always and proves that she can be funny and carry dramatic weight in the same film. I have always considered Bryce Dallas Howard to be a very accomplished actress and hopefully her scathing, pointed work will be recognized. Octavia Spencer and Jessica Chastain find a tender chemistry all their own. I have to say that \\"The Help\\" really, truly surprised me. I have never read the mega-selling novel from which it's based but I can tell you the film is a beautiful testament to American history and is a great display for some truly fine acting. For those who are turned off by sermonizing, preachy, obnoxious message movies (like myself), \\"The Help\\" is far from that. This is a tender film about real emotions and a subject that is rarely discussed in film today.ions and a subject that is rarely discussed in film today.
B00005LIN0|4.0|I'm a huge lover of opera, but I find I mainly connect in the opera house at a live performance, flaws, audience noise, uncomfortable seat and all. There, perhaps once or twice in a good season in a good house, I may experience transcendental moments in which I am completely transported emotionally by an opera. Opera video really needs to be understood as a different albeit related art form. The best of the genre use the story-telling power of cinema to make the emotional connection while still preserving and not overwhelming the stagecraft of opera.    This opera video is less cinematic than some. The sets and costumes preserve the feel of a production for the stage. As expected in a stage production it is a little abstract and doesn't embrace a realistic treatment of the grinding squalor of Catfish Row. However, the performances of the principals and all of the cast and chorus are compelling, and the singing is first rate throughout with as good a handling of dialect as you are going to get out of an international cast. This is a respectable and faithful treatment of Gershwin's opera - yes, it's an opera, an important piece of classical operatic repertoire. The score, a work of genius, is well handled here.    The power of this piece derives from its story which is rooted in a particular people, time and place. Attempts to transpose the story to somewhere else or clean it up are invariably damaging to the emotional power of the thing. I have to say in this connection I think the decision made in this production to get rid of the goat cart was unfortunate. Porgy and Bess is completely a piece of opera verismo. The indignity of that goat cart is very much wanted to emphasize the brutality of Porgy's situation. Other than that I think this production made the wise choices to be faithful to the libretto, and was a job well done.    The London Phil plays impeccably as always and with decent respect for Gershwin's intentions - I think, having listened to period recordings of performances of other Gershwin works conducted by the composer - but if there is one regret here it is that the playing strives perhaps for too much polish; the poor dears don't seem quite to grasp where the score calls for rawness, or even absolute frenzy. Tempii drag a bit slow in some places. Perhaps they should have sent the horn section to New Orleans for a fortnight beforehand. Perhaps the ensemble is a bit too large to give a completely authentic treatment as opera. The London Philharmonic would overflow the average house's pit by nearly a factor of two. If these complaints seem somewhat on a par with what you hear some Russians say about Tchaikovsky as performed outside of St. Petersburg, so be it.    Well worth watching. Very strong performances. Very strong singing. The thing managed to reduce me to tears several times. Highly recommended as opera video.

0786715103|5.0|LTG Romeo Dallaire's memoirs of his ill-fated turn as commander of the UN mission in Rwanda during that country's genocide is the extremely balanced and heartfelt account of a soldier's horror at what he experienced, both in terms of the killing itself and the absolute apathy of an international community that stood by and let it happen.  I suppose the most Machiavellian among us might countenance arguments about how there were no compelling global security motives to become further involved in Rwanda, but for those of us who actually have hearts that beat in our chests, the thought of human beings being slaughtered, mostly by machete, at the rate of 1 every 11 seconds around the clock for 100 days alone is enough to justify involvement.<br /><br />In any case, Dallaire does an outstanding job at describing what he saw, what he did, and what he didn't do over the course of his year in Rwanda.  By reading some of the more negative reviews here, I see that the details of the workings (and non-workings) of the international system became boring for some readers, and the reliance on acronyms was tedious (imagine the alternative, though).  I'll cop to being an international affairs junkie with military experience, so I'm used to the acronyms and those same details about nation-states and international organizations are quite interesting to me.  I will also say, however, that I am a pretty severe critic of writing (regardless of content) and in my opinion, this man can write.  He can really, really write.  I found his prose to be extraordinarily powerful without going over the top, and there were more than a few points that I had to just put the book down and say, \\"Wow.\\"  I can understand how those who were looking for more of a Hotel Rwanda type story might have been disappointed, but given the fairly widely held opinion that the international community really dropped the ball when it came to Rwanda, it was great for me to read such a fair (though venomous) description of how that actually worked.<br /><br />Although this book definitely contains elements of LTG Dallaire's own personal struggles and demons regarding the genocide, and there are short vignettes of the human faces of the slaughter, the soul-searching and questions of good and evil don't necessarily jump off the page at you.  But if you're looking for an extremely well-written, humane account of how the world stood by while almost one million people were massacred, despite being informed of the situation time and time again both before and during the slaughter, I highly recommended Shake Hands With the Devil.at actually worked. B002G1YPH0|5.0|Quick summary:  * Excellent cooling. Infinitely better than the stock Intel cooler (at least the ones given with the Core i7).  * This thing is BIG. Make sure you have the space. Measure your case before buying it.  * Installation requires minimal tools (just a screwdriver) but plenty of patience. Don't expect to be done in 5 minutes.    Relevant system specs:    CPU: Core i7 930  Motherboard: X58A-UD3R rev 2  Case: Antec Three Hundred Illusion Black Steel ATX Mid Tower (with all the wires to the annoying LEDs in the fans cut)    I bought the machine a year ago and decided to upgrade the CPU cooler. The stock cooler just wasn't cutting it for me. I had driven down both the core voltage (1.25V -> 1.00V) and QPI voltage (forgot), and replaced the thermal compound on the stock cooler with Tuniq TX-2. I'm thinking of switching this out for Arctic Silver 5 in the near future, just to compare the two. Anyway, the temperatures (unoverclocked) were still high: the hottest core would idle at 46C and the coolest at 39C. After a year I was fed up, so I bought this cooler to try and fix the problem.      Installation:    This is really the only downside to this CPU cooler. It took me about an hour (although I was distracted so this isn't quite a fair judgment). It is probably much easier to install this cooler before installing the motherboard. It requires placing a backplate, which is infinitely easier without the case in the way. Also, the instructions are not for the faint of heart. The diagrams are helpful, but the words essentially amount to: \\"install backplate\\" and \\"install cooler\\".     In my actual system, the backplate hits the case. I had to bend back some of the backplate (the parts for the installation on an AMD socket) to make it fit without pushing against the case (not the outer wall but the part of the chassis that holds the motherboard). Just in case, I added some extra electric tape to help prevent shorting, although I doubt this is going to be an actual problem.     With the backplate in place, the next fun part is installing the cooler. It is massive, so installing it with all the other parts in place is tricky unless you have a 2 year old's hands. I ended up having to use pliers just to get the fan's connection the motherboard in. The cooler is also held in place in a weird way. The cooler isn't screwed directly to the motherboard via the backplate. Instead, you have to put a little frame between the surface that touches the CPU and the heatsink, and then screw the frame down tightly to the screws mounted in the backplate.      Tightness:    The cooler is held to the CPU by a combination of screws and springs, meaning that the cooler is held very tightly against the processor. This is clearly much better than the pin/clip design of the stock Intel cooler, which only loosely holds on to the CPU.       Cooling:    For an air cooled system, although I have little to compare it to, I am very pleased. In a room at 22C (72F) my system now idles much colder than it used to. I have overclocked the system and raised some of the voltages, so the numbers I am giving here are not quite comparable. Although I have run Prime95 for 30 minutes to check system stability, I have not yet run it overnight to test it fully.    VCore: ~1.28V  QPI voltage: somewhere around 1.2V  Base clock: 160MHz (up from the default 133MHz I used to run at)  Core Multiplier: 22 (although I have a lot of settings turn on that drop the multiplier when not necessary)  Memory multiplier: 10 (running 1600MHz memory)  Uncore multiplier: 20 (I read that this should be double the memory multiplier)    Idle:    Clock rate: 1920MHz  Hottest core: 41C  Coldest core: 34C    Full Load (Prime95 with 8 workers):    Clock rate: 3360MHz - 3520MHz (it can't make up its mind between 21x and 22x)  Hottest core: 75C  Coldest core: 67C    As far as I can tell, these are great temperatures, meaning that the cooler is really doing its job.

B004LWZW4M|5.0|The Review:<br /><br />There have been many larger than life heroes of the silver screen. Kurt Douglas' Spartacus, Russell Crowes' Gladiator, and Mel Gibson's Braveheart. Now to that august company of heroes must be added Andy Serkis' Caesar from Rise of the Planet of the Apes. But Caesar is unlike any of the others. Though he is every bit as heroic, and in many ways more complex, Caesar is a chimpanzee.<br /><br />Rise of the Planet of the Apes is the prequel to the 1968 classic, The Planet of the Apes starring Charleton Heston. Rise tells the story of how it came to pass that our simian cousins skipped ahead of us on the evolutionary ladder. The story of Rise is told in such a way that it fits perfectly within the continuity of the original film. By the way, I highly recommend seeing the original as it really is one of the pillars of dystopian sci-fi. When you watch it, keep in mind that it was made more than 40 years ago and how cutting edge it must have seemed to that audience in terms of both story and special effects.<br /><br />After seeing the trailers for Rise, I expected a good movie. What I got was a great one and it is my favorite movie of the year so far. From its opening sequence to the closing credits, Rise hits all the high points for which storytellers strive. While I thoroughly enjoyed Thor, Captain America and X-Men First Class, Rise had an emotional impact well beyond what I experienced with those other great movies.<br /><br />Before the end of Rise you will come to know and love Caesar, a combination of computer generated imagery and the movements of actor, Andy Serkis, who also did the movement for King Kong in Peter Jackson's amazing film of the same name. Serkis' performance is all the more amazing since it's composed almost entirely of his motion captured facial expressions and body movement. Not many actors can do their best work under such restrictions. Andy Serkis is in a class by himself in this regard.<br /><br />Rise plays on our collective fascination with primates other than ourselves. Chimpanzees are eerily like us. They have personalities, live in groups and even wage war against other groups of chimpanzees. The ability of Rise to tell a story, in which apes and chimpanzees are every bit the star that their human counterparts are, is a rather amazing bit of film making. This is obviously the result of a lot of special effects, but I never once saw anything \\"fake\\" about the film's FX. Yes, intellectually I know that chimps were not taking direction or immersing themselves in the method school of acting. But damned if every scene didn't look as if real animals were on screen. Only King Kong and Avatar have ever made me forget I was looking at computer generated effects the way Rise did.<br /><br />Rise of the Planet of the Apes has as much action as any of the blockbusters of the summer of 2011, but it has a lot more heart. Caesar is part superhero, part freedom fighter and part Moses. If that sounds ridiculous, trust me, it isn't. Rise, by focusing on a non human character manages to explore the very essence of heroism, and our desire to be free. In many ways Caesar is as \\"human\\" as any of us. And on that somewhat corny note, the only negative thing I have heard about this movie was from a co-worker, who said he heard that it was \\"corny.\\" Well, I am as jaded as almost anyone in the concrete jungle, but I feel sorry for anyone who can't let themselves enjoy the awesomeness that is Rise of the Planet of the Apes because it's no longer cool to let a movie move you. For the rest of us-hail Caesar!!<br /><br />FTC Advisory: We purchased our own tickets.han ourselves. Chimpanzees are eerily like us. 1590171993|5.0|This is the story of one man's metamorphis throughout his life. In order to have understanding, one must read this book with knowledge of the story's timeline. ( Not much was known about mental illness, marriage was the ultimate committment, most people lived in America on farms, and women's roles were as homemaker-- their rights were not considered to be of importance.)   Without being born pre 1960, I believe this would be a difficult read.  But looking at this novel from 2 perspectives, that of today, and yesteryear, is what makes this novel so rich and interesting.     This book could easily be the story of any person's life, and the interest is in how the main character handles his predicaments and passions, and his growth throughout the book.   Like everyone else, some things he is active about, some things he is passive about, and some issues he cannot resolve at all, while others issues stay on the backburner until his growth-of-character allows him to act.   The book is about the life of a man raised on a farm who becomes an English professor.   His wife's mental illness is the ever-present background for his life.   Both he and his daughter become sacrificial lambs to his wife's illness, which in many ways, prevents him from fulfilling himself, and ultimately appears to destroy his daughter.  As a misunderstanding person of his time, he deals with his wife's craziness the best he can, but still manages to blame himself for her unhappiness.  Of course, it is easy to say from today's perspective, that he shouldn't have blamed himself, what he could have, should have done.  But to look within the knowledge of his time, he was coping the best way he knew how.    My favorite part of the book, is later in the book, when he learns how to defeat his old rival.  He develops a boldness  that never was a part of his younger years.  He fearlessly and intelligently decides that he will teach what he loves, and will not let anything stand in his way.  And he finds a wayto do it.  This is what his true heroism is all about.  He becomes his own hero by dropping his fears, and defeats the enemy by meeting him head on.    The character's true growth is at the end of the book, when dying, he reflects on his failures,and realizes that failures don't matter.  When failures are taken out of the equation, he has really had a good life.

B003Y5H5C4|3.0|THIS DOES CONTAIN SOME SPOILERS<br /><br />Ruben Fleischer's first directorial effort, Zombieland, was met with both critic acclaim and fan appraisal. There seem to be far fewer people who disliked Zombieland compared to those who enjoyed it. Zombieland was quite entertaining though; its cast was incredibly well-rounded, it offered a new spin on a story we've seen dozens of times before, and most of all it offered humor and sharp wit during what is otherwise the end of the world; a time when you least expected it. Zombieland was something special. However, 30 Minutes or Less seems like a completely different type of comedy. The trailers lead you to believe it could go either way; one way being extremely hilarious and the other ending with you kicking yourself because you didn't just wait to rent it. Even after seeing the movie, 30 Minutes or Less still manages to fall somewhere in the middle of that generalization.<br /><br />30 Minutes or Less starts off pretty strong. The dialogue is really funny, you can feel the chemistry between the actors, and the storyline is progressing at a steady pace. It seems like it's on track to be this fantastically amusing R-rated comedy that's one of the funniest films of the year, but it begins to lose steam after the bank robbery. It's like the humor begins to lose its wit and becomes more about bickering between the duos Nick (Jesse Eisenberg) and Chet (Aziz Ansari) and Dwayne (Danny McBride) and Travis (Nick Swardson). By the end of the movie, you'll wonder how a pizza deliveryman who does nothing but smoke pot, drink, play video games, and watch action movies who's never amounted to anything his entire life can rob a bank, get away with it, and be as smart as he is about the entire situation. Is it years of untapped potential finally bubbling to the surface or a giant plot hole? You'll have to be the judge of that.<br /><br />There seems to be a plethora of plot devices added in the second half of the movie that hurts it overall. A pen gun, a hired gun getting his own side story, a romance, the potential tan salon Dwayne and Travis won't stop talking about, police chases, the leverage changing hands several times, and flamethrowers all entering the picture, it all becomes too much to process. Trying to juggle that many scenes only results in a movie like this stumbling in its attempted execution.<br /><br />The cast is 30 Minutes or Less finest asset. The writing certainly has its moments, but the actors are what make the movie as entertaining as it is. Jesse Eisenberg portrays that same sharp wit you've come to expect from the actor. Meanwhile Danny McBride gives one of his best performances. McBride tends to be utilized so well as a supporting character. All of his qualities seem to shine brightest when he's not the main character. It's one of the reasons Your Highness didn't work out. Nick Swardson plays up the bomb expert/second fiddle to Dwayne role incredibly well. He's more intelligent than he lets on and the scene where he's unleashing his kicks in front of a tied up and bomb straddled Nick is probably his funniest in the movie. Last but not least, there's Michael Pe&ntilde;a who's the one character in the movie who can get laughs just by the way he talks alone. His \\"pimp\\" scene in the mirror is quite amusing. You may have noticed I left out Aziz Ansari and that's mostly because it felt like he wasn't very funny at all. He had a few one-liners that are snicker-worthy, but seemed to ruin it by running it into the ground and just overdoing it in general.<br /><br />The other disheartening thing about 30 Minutes or Less is the way it ends. It doesn't feel like a conclusion since everything just kind of stops. The movie ends with a laugh and has a pretty ridiculous scene after the credits, but it leaves so many unanswered questions. It was probably left open for a possible sequel, which would probably just completely ruin what limited appeal this comedy has.<br /><br />30 Minutes or Less is fast paced, action packed, and even downright hilarious at times. Unfortunately, it doesn't keep up the momentum it first starts off with. The humor becomes stale, the actors tend to get caught up in being silly rather than moving the story along, and action takes a front seat in the second half while the comedy takes a hit, but 30 Minutes or Less is still fairly amusing and at least worth a rental in the long run.ilarious at times. 0425241505|3.0|I've seen practically nothing but rave reviews for Dragon Bound by Thea Harrison, so I'm sorry to say that I was a little underwhelmed. I didn't hate it, and certain parts of it were fantastic, but I have enough complaints that I'm going to have to label it as average.<br /><br />The book opens with Pia, our heroine, escaping after stealing an item from Dragos' lair. This feat was supposed to have been impossible, but Pia has some hidden talents--and furthermore, she was threatened and blackmailed into the act. Dragos is beyond pissed at the insult of being robbed, and pursues Pia as she flees into the territory of his enemies. To say more would give too much away, so I'll just say that with Dragos being the leader of the Wyrs (shapeshifters, I guess), he has some pretty nasty enemies.<br /><br />I'm going to start with the world building, because the concept of a book containing dragons, elves, and fairies as main characters is what drew me to this book in the first place. Books with such a variety of supernatural creatures and concepts often suffer from info dump and exposition overload. Thankfully, this one wasn't too bad--the author keeps it simple. I actually wished for a little more information at certain times--some clarification on the distinctions between fae and elf and so on, for example. But overall, the world building is pretty solid.<br /><br />That said, there isn't anything spectacularly innovative going on in this book. It follows a lot of the traditional patterns you'll see in paranormal romance, and uses many familiar tropes. If you swapped out the dragon and other mythological creatures for vampires and werewolves, every reviewer would be calling this a boring cliched flop. And the thing is, you really could make that swap without damaging the plot too much.<br /><br />Let's take Dragos as an example. Initially I was impressed by the fact that he acted so much like the mythological dragon I hoped for. He's acquisitive and possessive, violent and uncivilized, a leader but a loner, and of course ancient and immortal. But other than the actual turning into a dragon, the above traits are ones I'm used to seeing in the vamps and werewolf alphas of other books. He just isn't unique in my mind. He's a nifty concept, but his personality is underdeveloped and unoriginal. As a result, I never fully connected with him.<br /><br />Then there's Pia. I liked Pia, because she seemed to act, and react to events, much the way I would expect a rational person to. She isn't too perfect, she has plenty of  moments where she freaks out. She makes some bad decisions, but still demonstrates competence. Throughout most of the book, it's a mystery as to what Pia actually is. The payoff is not disappointing.<br /><br />The timeline of the book spans a little over a week. That's a pretty short time for two people to fall in love and commit to one another. That leads me into my biggest issue with this book. I'm just going to say it, SPOILER though it is...this is a fated mate book. The relationship is destined to be, no point fighting it, don't know if they could live without one another, etc. The mate bond (in this book) is pretty much entirely used as a substitute for a real emotional connection, a contrived way of putting two people together who would probably not commit to one another otherwise. The sex scenes were sensual and intense, so I believed that Pia and Dragos have the hots for one another. And yes, I guess I believe they're bonded as mates because the book tells me so. But I'm not satisfied that they feel real, absolute love. I'm not even satisfied that they know each other very well. This book felt like the prelude to a real love story, not the love story itself.<br /><br />Overall, I'm not hugely disappointed in this book. I would probably read the next book in the series if I came across it. I just feel pretty neutral toward this one. 2.5 stars.r, and of course ancient and immortal. His three best friends are his ex-girlfriend, a hyperactive neurotic, and a one-named shut-in (it took a few more episodes for Kramer to evolve than the other characters). Additionally, the special features make all of the Seinfeld sets better than most shows. This is a good start to what became a legendary sitcom.

B002T9H2ME|5.0|Unfortunately, Science Fiction cinema, and literature have always had a somewhat difficult relationship. The literature has always been a rich field of ideas and thought experiments of every shape, but the cinematic forms tended towards the schlocky and gimmicky, with a few notable exceptions. In the literary world of speculative fiction, in which thousands upon thousands of short stories and novels have explored virtually every possible scenario in potential and alternative futures for humans and other types of creatures, there is a vast library of tropes that are commonly interwoven time and again in different configurations as a kind of exploration of the realm of possibility. Yet rarely has this been the case with cinematic Science Ficton. This film, however, happens to resemble the literary tradition more than the cinematic norms. There are no chases, and there is only one fight. But they do take a couple of very common tropes of speculative fiction and explore them in a very evocative and lyrical way--and in the process, ask some very profound questions about the nature of human rights, the social contract, community, competition, and collaboration; the nature of corporate labor, freedom, and the kind of complex problems that might go along with the ability to clone humans the way we clone apple trees.What happens when you reduce the meaning of a human being to the work they are capable of doing, or the job that needs to be done? What of their dreams? Their longings? Their hopes? Does any corporation care about the anxieties of their workers??? What about the customers of those corporations? Like you. Like me. Do you care about the hopes of the Chinese laborers who probably built the parts to the computer you are now staring at? Do you care about their safety? Their dormitory conditions--or their separation from loved ones? I'm not sure why we should--except that it makes me squeamish to think my purchasing power supports a system of slave labor and environment degradation that circles the globe.    I won't say much more, but, even though there is very little that is strictly original here--in literary speculative fiction there is literally nothing new under the sun--nothing that has not been thought of before--and probably 60 years ago at that--however, they do acknowledge their indebtedness to to certain traditions in a playful and respectful fashion. Also, kudos to both the set design and the cinematography, which were very authentic feeling and believable. Every work of science fiction can unravel if you tug at any of the threads too hard--you almost always have to accept a few basic premises--but, having done that, the story should hold together consistently and logically within that framework--and Moon does that, to a surprising degree, considering how often cinematic science fiction disappoints. This film deserves to be more widely known and is worthy of praise from even the most die-hard critic of speculative fiction.  I would have loved to see what Harlan Ellison would have had to say about Moon back when he was still reviewing films for the Magazine of Fantasy and Science Fiction. It rekindles my hope that maybe someday film makers will become educated enough to begin harvesting the nearly infinite fields of plenty that literary science fiction has ripened during the past 70 years. There is no excuse for any more ignorant schlock ever again. This is an example of how it can be done, folks.

And it's written centuries ago. Therefore it's very different from western novels. Everything is concise, succinct, and to the point. The pacing is lightening fast, and doesn't stop. Don't expect it to burst into a lengthy description of a scenery or somebody's appearance or anything really. Most of the time the reader has to imagine for themselves what everything looks like. If it does touch on a character's appearance, you can bet that character is important later on, which can be difficult to know with the rainstorm of characters introduced.<br /><br />With such a pace and length, you can already imagine that it covers an enormously long and very complicated plot with hundreds of characters. A myriad of the bravest, most capable, shrewdest, most intelligent, or most ambitious people dance across the scene. 0375701427|4.0|Having never read a novel by Roth (and arriving a little late in the game now that he has 30 novels behind him), I wasn't sure exactly what to expect with this novel, which I read for a book club. Most of my impressions of Roth were formed by what I'd heard about Portnoy's Complaint, the heavy emphasis on sex and Jewish angst, and finally seeing the film of Goodbye, Columbus. What I found, surprisingly, was a very thorough exposition of American experience from pre-World War II to the early 90's, focusing primarily on the crises of the sixties and seventies, particularly as they impact the life of the main character Seymour \\"Swede\\" Levov. The story is told by Nathan Zuckerman, Roth's fictional author and alter ego who, aside from briefly reappearing about mid-way into the novel, disappears altogether. In a mode similar to Balzac's depiction of the book publishing industry in Lost Illusions and Melville's treatment of the whaling industry in Moby-Dick, Roth presents an extremely detailed description and background of the glove-making industry in 20th century America. The focal character is a Jewish Golden Boy, Swede Levov, the handsome, universally loved athlete from Jewish Newark, NJ, who had everything going for him and whose stage was set for a perfect, and perfectly successful, life of affluence. Unfortunately, the stage was also set for extreme personal tragedy and disaster in the form of his daughter Merry who, as told almost from the first page, went berserk at sixteen, became a runaway, got herself involved with a terrorist group protesting not only the Vietnam War in early '68 but the entire American way of life as exemplified most completely by her father. The first hundred pages or so, the point is made ad infinitum that this little demon ruined the Swede's life and that of the rest of his family and brought a plague of disaster and nightmare upon her parents. Zuckerman repeats this fact about five times and fifty pages too much before stepping into the background and letting the Swede's life unfold so that we can begin to unravel the mystery of this perfect young Jew, who later becomes a perfect modern Job. Personally, having read and enjoyed many of the long-winded, nineteenth century novels, I did not mind the numerous divergences from the `plot'. However, there are loose ends that are never tied and the last fifty or so pages derail the plot and dilute the impact that had been building progressively. This is a major disappointment considering the power of much of what had preceded it. It's as though Roth were revealing clues in a mystery and then, just as all of the clues were presented, he just got tired of going down that track and backpedaled for the last several pages to reach an oblique and weak conclusion that ends with an overstatement of one of the themes and never resolves what happened on the level of the reality of the characters' lives. This strikes me as an unfinished novel. It is infuriating because it is so powerful in many respects but the first portion could have been tightened as well as many passages throughout the rest of it and a complete conclusion as thorough as the detailed backgrounds of Swede, his wife Dawn and other characters could have been reached without increasing the length of the novel substantially.

B000100ENC|5.0|Between 1970 and 1975 Black Sabbath released six great albums that today are classics, in every sense of this overused word. Afterwards the things went very badly indeed. The uncommonly dull ''Technical Ecstasy'' (1976), as thoroughly un-Sabbath as its futuristic cover, and the simply dismal ''Never Say Die'' (1978), which sounds pretty much like cheap copy of the Beatles on steroids, marked the sad end of a great era in the history of rock music. But every band deserves to be judged - if at all - by their best. If we concede that ''Black Sabbath'' (1970) and ''Sabotage'' (1975), the debut and the beginning of the end respectively, fine albums as they may be, do not quite match Sabbath's own extraordinarily high standards, we are left with the four albums in the middle: ''Paranoid'' (1970), ''Master of Reality'' (1971), ''Vol. 4'' (1972) and ''Sabbath Bloody Sabbath'' (1973).<br /><br />To put the matter bluntly, I personally wouldn't want to be without any of these six albums and would give them all five full stars, despite some mild reservations I may occasionally have. They are utterly different and every one of them has its own unique character. But if I am forced to choose but one Sabbath album from the Ozzy years, I would most probably go with ''Sabbath Bloody Sabbath''.<br /><br />Whether ''Sabbath Bloody Sabbath'' is your favourite album or not, like virtually everything else, is purely a matter of taste. But it is beyond dispute that this is their most sophisticated, varied and musically mature album. It was inevitable, perhaps, that it should turn to be the pinnacle of their discography from the 1970s. Indeed, nothing like it can be found among the greatest rock music of the time, with the possible exception of Led Zeppelin's Fourth album and ''Physical Graffiti''. There are but eight songs in ''Sabbath Bloody Sabbath'', but all deserve a few words of their own.<br /><br />''Sabbath Bloody Sabbath'' opens the album with one of Tony's most unforgettable riffs. Curiously, the song has never become a concert favourite, even though it would certainly make a fine opportunity for terrific headbanging. The second part of the song, in particular, is one of the slowest and heaviest things ever recorded by Sabbath. The first part, on the other hand, can boast a marvellous lyrical passage.<br /><br />''A National Acrobat'' is an entirely different affair, as often happens in this album. It's a complex three-part composition with some of Tony's most whimsical guitar work, especially the obsessive riff-solo towards the end. The intense middle part is in somewhat stark contrast with the buoyant outer ones. It includes most of the text which, mostly, matches the quality of the music, even if after-life speculations are not your cup of tea:<br /><br />Just remember love is life<br />And hate is living death<br />Treat your life for what it's worth<br />And live for every breath<br />Looking back I've lived and learned<br />But now I'm wondering<br />Here I wait and only guess<br />What this next life will bring<br /><br />''Fluff'' is no mere fluff, as some superficial fellows seem to think. It's a most beautiful instrumental in which at least three different guitars, all played by Tony of course, are ingeniously combined; there is only bass and a hint of keyboards to keep them company. The piece has serenity and tranquility that are not often associated with Black Sabbath. Yet ''Fluff'' has a distinguished pedigree: ''Orchid'' from ''Master of Reality'' and ''Laguna Sunrise'' from ''Vol. 4''. Like the album on the whole, this perfectly delightful instrumental is the pinnacle in its category.<br /><br />''Sabbra Cadabra'' is the kind of stuff that's so uniquely Sabbath that it can hardly bear any other treatment. It was a great mistake on Metallica's side to try it, and even guitar magician such as Kirk Hammett couldn't pull off convincingly the wacky major riff. The lyrics are some of the most outrageously flippant in whole album,recounting Ozzy's sexual escapades, but with such stupendous music who cares? This is also the song with most prominent keyboards in the whole album, and it is here that Rick Wakeman himself makes a memorable appearance.<br /><br />''Killing Yourself to Live'' is one of the more straightforward tracks, pretty heavy but with a great variety of quite outstanding guitar riffs and solos. Indeed, this is perhaps the most complex composition in the whole album, having at least three clearly defined sections none of which, except the first, includes any repetition. In earlier times, Sabbath might have made at least two songs of this material.<br /><br />''Who Are You?'' could not have been more different than its predecessor. It's a somber and restrained song full of bizarre sounds and virtually without any guitar riffs or solos. The text is one of the most serious and shattering in the whole album. It takes some time to realize that this is actually Ozzy's conversation with God himself. I guess He won't be amused:<br /><br />Yes I know the secret<br />That's within your mind<br />You think all the people<br />Who worship you are blind<br />You're just like Big Brother<br />Giving us your trust<br />And when you have played enough<br />You'll just cast our souls<br />Into the dust<br />Into the dust<br /><br />You thought that it would be easy<br />From the very start<br />Now I've found you out<br />I don't think you're so smart<br />I only have one more question<br />Before my time is through<br />Please I beg you tell me<br />In the name of hell<br />Who are you?<br />Who are you?<br /><br />''Looking For Today'' is a most welcome relief: cheerful stuff with one of Tony's most ebullient riffs. There is more in it, though. ''Looking for Today'' shares with the title song the highly effective idea of introducing a beautiful and tranquil passage, entirely different mood, in the middle of the guitar maelstrom. The contrast, as in the other case, works wonderfully.<br /><br />''Spiral Architect'' is a most fitting conclusion for it is the craziest song here. Especially the lyrics are quite a mish-mash of science fiction, fantasy and pure nonsense. I and a friend of mine used to joke that these ''silver ships on plasmic oceans'' were inspired by fag ends floating in beer. You know, there is a great story - apocryphal or not - how this text was written. Apparently, Ozzy was out of inspiration and called Geezer. The bassist told him to call back in half an hour. He did and was told the complete text. Ozzy loved it so much that he couldn't but ask: ''What did you smoke, man?!'', to which Geezer replied innocently ''Ah, nothing, I wrote it on the grass outside.'' ''Do you happen to sell this grass? Can I buy it'', Ozzy immediately inquired. No matter how strange the text, the music is definitely of the same stupendous quality as the rest of the album.<br /><br />My only qualm with ''Sabbath Bloody Sabbath'' is actually the usual one I have with all of Sabbath's early albums: a little too much guitar dubbing, particularly of solos. A very minor point of course. The album is very well recorded sonically, indeed better than any Sabbath album before (or since indeed, with the possible exception of ''Heaven and Hell'', but that's quite another story).<br /><br />Musically and artistically, ''Sabbath Bloody Sabbath'' is an essential listening for anybody interested in rock music from those heady times - the 1970s - which will never again occur. You have to listen to it at least once, even if it's only to know that it's not your cup of tea.s is also the song with most prominent keyboards in the whole album, and it is here that Rick Wakeman himself makes a memorable appearance. In earlier times, Sabbath might have made at least two songs of this material.    ''Who Are You?'' could not have been more different than its predecessor. It's a somber and restrained song full of bizarre sounds and virtually without any guitar riffs or solos. The text is one of the most serious and shattering in the whole album. It takes some time to realize that this is actually Ozzy's conversation with God himself. B000002KDO|5.0|This is a very odd parallel, but it seems to me that ''Master of Reality'' (1971) is to Sabbath's discography what ''Reign in Blood'' (1986) is to Slayer's. Of course in the meantime - mere 15 years - heavy metal changed quite a bit, especially in terms of sound. Yet both albums were third for the bands and both were linked with extremes: only where Slayer speeded up to the limits of the possible, Sabbath slowed down more than ever before or since. And they got heavier, too. By modern standards, or even by those of the early 1980s, ''Master of Reality'' is light stuff. But consider the year of its release: 1971! This is the year of Led Zeppelin's classic ''IV'' and Deep Purple's ''Fireball'', both of them conspicuously lighter fare (which, of course, has no relation to their musical value). I wonder what a shock for the world of rock music something like ''Master of Reality'' must have been in 1971. Well, whatever the case was, the album has aged fantastically well.    This is Sabbath's shortest and less varied album, yet it is one of the most unique in terms of sound and atmosphere. It is, in fact, haunting. There are only eight songs here, and two of them are short instrumentals (''Embryo'' and ''Orchid''). The rest six, however, consist of five impressive compositions and one unusually subdued ballad. The latter is ''Solitude'' and it does require quite a feat of imagination to recognize Ozzy's voice. The song has its predecessor in the even more bizarre ''Planet Caravan'' from ''Paranoid'' (1970), but it is rather more successful. The rest are all classics. ''Sweet Leaf'', with its landmark cough in the beginning - reportedly Tony choked while smoking some low-quality joint - and the dazzling ''Children of the Grave'', the fastest track here, have been concert favourites more or less since 1971. (Note the monstrously heavy sound of the slow part in the latter: without analog in the rock music of the early 1970s.) ''Lord of This World'' and ''Into the Void'' are the slowest tracks here (apart from one very fast section in the latter). This is the sound that has become all but Sabbath's trademark and it is not for nothing that it is considered to have played some part in the development of the so called ''doom metal''. Distorted riffs, tortured solos, crushing bass, wailing vocals - there is plenty of everything any fan of the early Sabbath might want.    The album is particularly well recorded, too. The constant attempts of Tony and Geezer to outplay each other have seldom been captured with such clarity. Bill's drums and Ozzy's voice come out powerfully but very well blended with the guitars and the bass. And one last point: the lyrics once again prove, if any proof is needed, that Black Sabbath never really had anything to do with puerile stuff like Satanism. Their texts may not be profound, but they aren't concerned with black magic either. Instead, an ode to joint (''Sweet Leaf''), a warning about the future (''Children of the Grave''), religious speculations (''After Forever'', ''Lord of This World'') and something like science fiction (''Into the Void'') are the themes here.    In short, terrific early Sabbath well worth the time and the attention of anybody seriously interested in rock music from the 1970s. Sabbath fans, of course, will have acquired the album long time ago. If there are any young fellows who are fascinated by some of the classic albums produced by Black Sabbath during the Ozzy era, but haven't yet heard ''Master of Reality'', I suggest they hasten to rectify this.

1594202710|5.0|Now 88 years old, one of the twentieth century's pre-eminent diplomats dips once more into the formidable store of experience accumulated since his first visit to China some 40 years ago and offers up a rich hybrid of memoir, autobiography and foreign policy analysis on China.<br /><br />With the definitive emergence of China as heir apparent to superpower status and its growing global economic and political clout, the timing of this book could not be better. It should be of interest not only to students of international relations and diplomacy, but to a broader audience of readers both in China and the West seeking a better understanding of the thinking behind the policies of China in a rapidly globalizing world.<br /><br />\\"On China\\" begins with a lengthy but fascinating look at the early history of the Middle Kingdom stretching back four millennia, during which China regarded itself as the centre of the globe and saw its own culture and traditions as the highest forms of human attainment. It describes the national humiliation represented by the European intervention beginning in the late 18th century that imposed terms of trade and a general opening up of the empire to the outside world. Much of China's current foreign and domestic policies, in particular its obsession with national unity and stability and its rejection of anything that can be construed as interference in the country's domestic affairs, can be explained, according to Kissinger, by the wounds caused by that period.<br /><br />The narrative continues with the period of national consolidation under Mao Zedong and the opening up to the United States continued by Deng Xiaoping, a process in which the writer was a key participant. Kissinger's narrative is enriched by the numerous first-hand accounts of his meetings with virtually all of the Chinese leaders from Mao Zedong to Hu Jintao and by his access to hitherto classified documents. The book ends with an overview of the current state of US-China relations in which he argues for a more nuanced foreign policy towards China by the United States that took account of the historical realities that shape China's attitudes towards the West, including the rejection of Western notions of liberal democracy and human rights. China's position was summed up in an \\"oral message\\" sent by its political leaders to then President Bush through Kissinger in September 1990:<br /><br />\\"For over a century, the Chinese people were all along subjected to bullying and humiliation by foreign powers. We do not want to see this wound reopened. I believe that as an old friend of China, Mr. President, you understand the sentiments of the Chinese people. China cherishes Sino-US friendly relations and cooperation which did not come easily, but it cherishes its independence, sovereignty and dignity even more.\\"<br /><br />Along the way, Kissinger offers trenchant observations of the players on the international stage. Gorbachev, for example, \\"had recognized the pervasive stagnation (of communism) but lacked the imagination or skill to break through its built-in rigidities.\\" Comparing the difference in personalities between Zhou Enlai and Mao Zedong, he wrote that \\"Mao dominated any gathering; Zhou suffused it. Mao's passion strove to overwhelm opposition; Zhou's intellect would seek to persuade or outmaneuver it; Mao was sardonic; Zhou penetrating. Mao thought of himself as a philosopher; Zhou saw his role as an administrator or a negotiator. Mao was eager to accelerate history; Zhou was content to exploit its currents. A saying he often repeated was `The helmsman must ride with the waves.' When they were together, there was no question of the hierarchy, not simply in the formal sense but the deeper aspect of Zhou's extraordinarily deferential conduct.\\"<br /><br />The reader may be forgiven for wondering to what extent Kissinger's current incarnation as an international consultant on all things China might be responsible for the generally reverential tone of this his sixteenth book. It is true that the narrative is suffused with a degree of romanticism that glosses over the excesses of the Cultural Revolution and the suppression of the student demonstrators at Tiananmen Square as secondary to the higher purpose of maintaining the stability of a system which in one generation has transformed a poverty-stricken nation into a global economic giant to which many a recession-plagued Western economy now looks for assistance. The senile and dribbling Chairman Mao, regarded in the end as a mad monster by many, including within the Chinese leadership circle, comes across in Kissinger's narrative as doddering in his later years but fearless, wise and benevolent.<br /><br />The heroes, if any, in this account are the generation of leaders of the post-Mao era steering a course between the perceived all-important need for unity and stability and the freedoms that would unleash the full creative energies of the Chinese people and thereby guarantee their future prosperity. Of these leaders, Deng Xiao Ping, who was thrice purged from power by Mao and whose son, Deng Pufang, was tormented by Red Guards and pushed off the roof of a building at Beijing University during the Cultural Revolution and then denied admission to hospital for his broken back, emerges as the transformative leader who challenged the orthodoxies bequeathed by Mao and set the Middle Kingdom on a new course towards modernity and prosperity.<br /><br />This book, the distillation of more than 40 years of engagement with China and some 50 visits, both public and private, to that country, helps to dispel some of the commonly held stereotypes about China and gives both the general public and decision-makers a more nuanced basis on which to make decisions or form judgements about the awakened giant.true that the narrative is suffused with a degree of romanticism that glosses over the excesses of the Cultural Revolution and the suppression of the student demonstrators at Tiananmen Square as secondary to the higher purpose of maintaining the stability of a system which in one generation has transformed a poverty-stricken nation into a global economic giant to which many a recession-plagued Western economy now looks for assistance. The senile and dribbling Chairman Mao, regarded in the end as a mad monster by many, including within the Chinese leadership circle, comes across in Kissinger's narrative as doddering in his later years but fearless, wise and benevolent. Of these leaders, Deng Xiao Ping, who was thrice purged from power by Mao and whose son, Deng Pufang, was tormented by Red Guards and pushed off the roof of a building at Beijing University during the Cultural Revolution and then denied admission to hospital for his broken back, emerges as the transformative leader who challenged the orthodoxies bequeathed by Mao and set the Middle Kingdom on a new course towards modernity and prosperity. Salmon are, of course, cold-water fish that are challenging to catch with a rod and reel but taste all the better once caught. So, we're on the same page, yes?<br /><br />Now consider the chances of finding a novel that adroitly mixes not just Yemen and salmon fishing but also the British Parliament, Al Qaeda, a mystical sheikh, the art of public relations, a sad love story, and a journey of self-discovery. Before I read this book, I would have defied anyone to accomplish that seemingly impossible task. Written when he was 59 years old at the end of a successful business careeer, the book reportedly allowed him to write about what he knows best (as every teacher urges in Creative Writing 101). B002HMHROQ|3.0|Upon its release, \\"The Blue Mask\\" was meant to signal the beginning of a new phase in Reed's music (the sober one) as well as a return to form in his songwriting. It certainly is the first but falls short on the second count.     Reed spends a considerable amount of time rediscovering traditional values (home, love, marriage!) and villifying both booze and drugs. Lyrically, then, the air is often heavy with conservative cliches and humorless observations. Musically, however, Reed doesn't botch the whole thing. The title track, \\"My House,\\" and \\"The Gun\\" reflect brilliant songwriting that is also successful in changing the style which had driven him to a musical standstill in the late 70's. Here is a bones and flesh approach whose directness and simplicity only highlight the virtues of the songs. Robert Quine is particularly provocative in developing a guitar sound that is both sparse and harrowing at times. The same goes for the standout (and sometimes Pastorius-like) bass playing of Fernando Saunders. His interplay with the guitars is pivotal in moving the songs in the right direction.     Having said this, the rest of the songwriting is lackluster and sometimes embarrassingly schmaltzy, as is the case with \\"Heavenly Arms,\\" a song even Stevie Wonder wouldn't sing without cringing. In fact, stylistic developments aside, this album is really no better than the two previous erratic albums put out by Reed, both of which suffer from the same inconsistency.     It is a matter of some wonder that much of Reed's material should be so overrated by fans who seem more fascinated by his dark past. And it is a matter of some irony that they should continue to overrate the man for the same reasons after he condemns his past in their face.  Go figure.

1451629184|5.0|One of the most amazing stories of modern times was Jaycee Dugard and her eighteen years of captivity. Cleverly timed to coincide with the release of \\"A Stolen Life,\\" it was nevertheless the story on people's minds since August of 2009.    The book reads extremely quickly.  Since it is so compelling and written in a spare (though often hauntingly lyrical) style, and it is not overlong, it is possible to read the book in one day.  Many reviewers report doing just this.  Early on, the book gets emotionally difficult in the weeks and months immediately following Jaycee's capture.  Without giving too many details away, people treat dogs better than she was treated.  The raw sexual details are revealed without blinking.  As readers we find out about the frequency and the length of Phillip Garrido's assaults on Jaycee's youth.      Every single question observers may have about Jaycee's plight are answered throughout her book.  How did she deal with the boredom and loneliness?  She became extraordinarily involved with her pets (wouldn't you have?)  She wrote journals and shares them with the reader, set on the pages as a copy from her own hand.  How did she give birth to two baby girls without medical assistance?  She goes into details about that, too.  At the end of the book, it is even revealed that both she and her daughters managed to stay healthy in every aspect without years of any medical or dental care.    It is apparent that Jaycee ferociously protects the privacy of her daughters.  Their names are never revealed and the details of their early development are only glanced upon.  This led some reviewers to erroneously conclude that Jaycee cared moreabout her pets than about her children!  Yet nothing is farther from the truth.    Jaycee Dugard has set up a foundation called JAYC (just ask yourself to care) which will help families dealing with reunification in circumstances similar to hers, and to families who have suffered other traumas along those lines.  And whether she knows it or not, her story stands as a metaphor for all the imprisoned, alienated, and violated of us and how we muster the courage to persevere.

B003Y5H57E|5.0|Let me start off my review of this film by saying that I hate Steve Carrell. Watching him on \\"The Office\\" for all those years was painful, because his character, and it seemed his acting style was unbelievably stupid and bland. While he has occasionally been a decent funnyman, I hadn't seen him in a role where he was remotely interesting and believable.    Yesterday, I was at the movies, pretty much the whole day, and decided to check out \\"Crazy Stupid Love\\", because it had decent critical and audience reactions, better than I expected for a romantic comedy, and it had some of my favorite people in it, including Julianne Moore, Emma Stone and Ryan Gosling. And, to say the least, I was caught by surprise.    One of the reasons for this, is because \\"Crazy Stupid Love\\" works as a romantic comedy and a drama. Most romantic comedies, I find, are predictable, stale, and essentially one carbon copy after another, of the same exact story, played out in a slightly altered way. You probably won't guess where \\"Crazy Stupid Love\\" is going. While the ending itself is alot like I predicted it, there's a big plot twist that presents itself in the climax that I really didn't see coming. And that's reason enough to see this movie.    However, there are many (better) reasons. One of the big reasons is that it avoids making any of its characters caricatures. Each character is real, and flawed, screwed up and deals with his or her problems in a very grounded and grown-up way.     It revolves around Cal (Carell), a man who has been married to Emily (Moore) for 25 years, and is rocked by the revelation that she wants a divorce, after sleeping with a co-worker (Kevin Bacon). Cal begins hanging out at a chic pickup bar, where he meets Jacob (Ryan Gosling), a lothario who takes a different woman home every night. He picks up on Cal's depression, and helps him turn into something of a womanizer. Meanwhile, Cal's floppy-haired thirteen-year-old son Robbie has a crush on his older babysitter,who has a little crush of her own. Also, Jacob meets Hannah (Emma Stone), a young lawyer who has heard every pickup line, and therefore, has a quite jaded outlook on love.    And it is the first film or TV show to portray Steve Carrell as a human being. He can act! Who knew? I actually liked him in this role, because I felt that he wasn't Michael Scott, or that guy from \\"Get Smart\\", or \\"The 40-Year-Old Virgin\\", but an average guy whose life didn't turn out as he had hoped. I bought him in this role. He more than keeps up with the rest of the film's stellar cast.    Another revelation here is Ryan Gosling. I've mostly seen him in depressing dramatic roles (\\"Blue Valentine\\", \\"The Notebook\\"), while here, he proves himself to be a cunning comedian. He has spectacular comedic timing, and clearly has a lot of fun here with the other actors. He seems to be the superficial funnyman at first, and, like the rest of the cast in this terrific film, opens up, and surprises the audience.    While Julianne Moore is always great, her character and Carrell's character mesh in a way that makes you believe that their marriage lasted 20+ years. Emma Stone is on fire right now, doing a great role in \\"The Help\\", she shines just as much here, if not more. Her character is instantly lovable, and I can't wait to see what she does next.     This film also features Marisa Tomei, who gives a fierce, and very funny performance as a recovering alcoholic schoolteacher who dates Cal for awhile after his divorce. Kevin Bacon also made me laugh, as Emily's co-worker, who was her post-divorce rebound. While these two have brief roles, they are very funny and make the most of what they are given.    What makes this film memorable for me, is that it is realistic. It doesn't paint love out to be this magical thing that will automatically make anyone happy. It knows that love doesn't always work out, and occasionally ends in heartbreak and/or violence. And while it's somewhat a cautionary tale, it ends hopefully and overall, kind of happily. It made me cry a little, and it made me laugh a lot. And while it could have been a bit longer, and given its characters some more room to grow, it was a satisfying overall package that I would recommend to someone who's trying to escape the summer of superhero flicks and sequels.

0062515675|4.0|Since I mentioned Robin Sharma and \\"The Monk Who Sold His Ferrari\\" in my latest video on my website (August 2011), people have asked me about his book. It was one of the first books that I read and recognized as spiritual fiction. The subtitle of this book is \\" a fable about fulfilling your dreams and reaching your destiny\\", and it truly is a fable and totally fictional. Initially when I read it I thought it was a true story until some of the things that happened to the lawyer Julian just seemed impossible to believe. I was really in the infancy of my spirituality at the time, however this story did get me hooked.<br /><br />This fable is about a very successful lawyer named Julian Mantle who is stricken by a massive heart attack in the middle of a packed courtroom. One of his proteges, John, is there to witness the big man crumple to the ground, and the story progresses from there. The author provides you with a detailed background of the circumstances leading up to Julian's heart attack, and you are then fast-forwarded to Julian's return after several years of being away from the rat race.<br /><br />Not only does Julian survive the heart attack, he quits the law firm, sells all of his possessions (including his red Ferrarai) and travels to India on an \\"expedition\\" to become a monk. This explains the title of the book. When he returns he visits John and meets with him several times to explain his \\"transformation\\" and to explain the lessons that he has learned.<br /><br />When Julian returns, he is virtually unrecognizable to John. He is lean, tanned and calm. In fact the only evidence left of the man that he once was is a scar on his chest from the surgery he had as a result of his heart attack. Over the course of several meetings, Julian explains how he was transformed by a meeting with the \\"Sages of Sivanna\\". These \\"Sages\\" teach Julian many things and show him that life is much more than material wealth and possessions.<br /><br />This is a spiritual fiction book and I have found it very easy to read and to keep interested in (I have read it four times). One of the things that I found unique (for it's time) was that it also includes an \\"Action Summary\\" and \\"Julian's Wisdom In a Nutshell\\" at the end of every chapter that has provided you with one of the \\"7 Timeless Virtues of Enlightened Living\\".  Many books today include a reader's study guide or group discussion outlines, however this was one of the first books I had encountered that did this in a fiction book.<br /><br />Written in 1997, this book is just as relevant today as it was then. With all the hype about \\"The Secret\\" and the fiction book \\"The Shack\\", Mr. Sharma brought all of the lessons that they have been teaching into his book at least ten years before they became popular. I would recommend that you read this book, particularly if you have read similar books and you will see that learning life lessons can be enjoyable when combined with an interesting story. I also recommend \\"Life Lessons From The Monk Who Sold His Ferrari\\" and I deem it essential to own and KEEP \\"Who Will Cry When You Die\\", both by Robin Sharma. B003EYVXTG|4.0|Winter's Bone is like Frozen River, another recent Oscar nominated film, in that it depicts the lives of poor, rural people; but easier to bear because of the distraction of solving the mystery of the father's whereabouts.     Debra Granik filmed in Missouri using local, non-professional actors in supporting roles. As someone who is from a rural area I appreciate seeing a story in this setting, void of caricature, with the details intact. In some ways Winter's Bone reminds me of Byambasuren Davaa's narrative documentaries about Mongolian nomads (e.g. Cave of the Yellow Dog, The Story of the Weeping Camel), where local people play themselves in fictional stories. Similarly, Granik's vision and attention to detail, and use of local talent, lets us see a place many of us don't know firsthand.    A highlight for me is Granik's depiction of the children, especially Ashlee, the 6yo girl. We get to know her almost exclusively by observing her playing and caring for animals, which captures the essence of a young child's way of being in the world in a way few films do.     This movie is partly about the toll of lack of economic opportunity and crystal meth in rural America, which is contextualized by the inclusion of bits of mountain culture (the people playing bluegrass at the party, the scene with Jessop's wood carving, and Teardrop playing the banjo) that show that these characters had something better before their lives slipped away from them.     Because of its depiction of people with an uncertain future in a place where it feels like there was once something better, this film reminds me in some ways of The Last Picture Show.

B003Y5H5A6|4.0|This is the best comic book movie to be released this summer. That is what it is, a comic book movie. It does not surpass that level but nobody ever said it had to. While not a Spider-man 2 or Dark Knight it fits in well as a first entry in an inevitable series. Perhaps not the fun, turn the formula on its head, film that Iron Man was, it is still an excellent comic book film that manages to have developed characters and a story actually propelled by plot rather than CG sequences (I'm looking at you Transformers 2 and 3). Still, even with all its positive attributes the film might've been largely a one time viewing for me, that is, if it hadn't been for Hayley Atwell as Peggy Carter.     My personal, and largest, issue with comic book movies has been their seeming inability to cast memorable and talented women in the leading lady spot. While the leading lady role has frequently been nothing more than a damsel in distress (other than perhaps in team up movies such as X-men), something unfortunately true even of Nolan's gritty Dark Knight films, here we find that is not the case. Peggy Carter is a female character who stands out amongst the many comic book movie female characters. She is strong willed, independent, can handle herself, never asks for help, doesn't take any crap and does it all in a period where women were undeniably treated as less than men. She does all these things yet she can still walk into a bar in the middle of the film wearing a red dress that leaves everyone at a loss for words.     Even better than Peggy Carter's character, and the reason I named the actress rather than the character in my review title, was the casting of Hayley Atwell in the role. The character could have become something much different without a capable actress to play the balance of tough yet feminine. Worse it could have been filled by some less capable big name actress for box office draw. For years in comic book movies I have either dreaded or at best barely stomached the casting of female leading roles.     Kirsten Dunst had her moments in the Spidey films but then would come the time when she had to be kidnapped and scream and holler for help, she also always needed a man in her life, if not Peter then another stand in to take his place. Batman Begins casting of Katie Holmes always seemed strange to me, why in a film full of lower status but credible actors would Nolan cast a more household name with much less credibility? Whatever the reason, she played the part but it is not the role anyone remembers from that film. The same goes for the sequel, in which I had high hopes Maggie Gyllenhaal would make welcome changes to the previously pitchy character, unfortunately I dare say that Rachel Dawes only became more helpless and became the one negative to be found in The Dark Knight.     More recently I was disappointed to find Gwenyth and Natalie both playing supposed strong women but one (Pepper Potts) who is subordinate to a man who treats her badly and still needs to be saved in the end and the other (Jane Foster) fairly shallow and school girlish overall.     While Jane only likes Thor at first because of his muscles, Peggy meets Steve Rogers/The Cap when he is a ninety pound weakling. We see her first tinge of interest in him when he is still this small, all based on his philosophies on fighting and dating. It is this established connection before he becomes a perfect male specimen that cements the story and keeps us truly engaged. Peggy and Steve truly bring out the best in eachother and actually share a common bond of struggling to break free from their respective stereotypes that allows the audience to believe in their relationship, one that is actually formed out of friendship first. The film is great and a wonderful starting point for a series, the ending is unique if only a plot device required for the Avengers tie in and I truly can't wait to see where they take Captain America in his sequel. He has by the end of the film, especially thanks to Peggy Carter, become quite a different hero with quite a different chip on his shoulders than any we have yet to see.     So I have wondered why comic book movies don't cast relatively unknown actresses to play these parts and allow them to really create a character. Finally Captain America has done just that with the lovely Hayley Atwell as Peggy Carter and I hope we see much more of it in future comic book films. The saddest part of Captain America and perhaps the thing that compells me to want to see it again Repeated times is that based on the ending it is very unlikely we will see the fantastic Peggy Carter/Hayley Atwell in all her glory in any of the sequels. They have some big shoes to fill when they inevitably try to create a Sharon Carter character, let's hope they take cues from however it was that they came to choose Hayley.

The good guys are going to start winning!\\" you turn around and some other awful thing happens to them.  There are plots within plots, aspects of the story that don't seem to real contribute to the overall plot arc, and then... Martin again kills off one of my favorite \\"good\\" characters.<br /><br />That just pisses me off.<br /><br />That said, A Dance With Dragons is still a good book, but it isn't the best in the series.  It moves the plot forward, but Arya's list of a-holes who need killing hasn't gotten much shorter and frankly, there are some other people who need to be added to it.  I'm still waiting for the Frey's to get their comeuppance, as well as for Ramsay the Bastard to have some one gut him, skin him, and feed his worthless, cruel, and villainous carcassto his own dogs.  Yes. I have some anger management issues.<br /><br />Now, I won't say who got killed, but I will say this: House Stark better come back with a fricken vengeance when this is all over.
